<div id="review_398627044" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/398627044" />
    <a title="TheBookSmugglers" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1223249-thebooksmugglers"><img alt="TheBookSmugglers" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1316717753p2/1223249.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/398627044?book_show_action=true">Aug 23, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="TheBookSmugglers" class="user" itemprop="url" name="TheBookSmugglers" href="/user/show/1223249-thebooksmugglers">TheBookSmugglers</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer398627044" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6818646376721348162">Originally reviewed on <a href='http://thebooksmugglers.com/2012/08/book-review-origin-by-jessica-khoury.html' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Book Smugglers</a><br/><br/>In the heart of the Amazon rainforest, there is a scientific lab and living compound called Little Cambridge. Little Cam houses some of the world's top scientific minds, including geneticists, biochemical engineers, botanists, and entomologists. <br/><br/>It also hides a girl named Pia - seventeen years old, immortal, and <em>perfect</em>. <br/><br/>The fruit of five generations of genetic experimentation and injection of a toxic, but immortality-giving extract from the rare bloom, e</span>
  <span id="freeText6818646376721348162" style="display:none">Originally reviewed on <a href="http://thebooksmugglers.com/2012/08/book-review-origin-by-jessica-khoury.html" rel="nofollow noopener">The Book Smugglers</a><br /><br />In the heart of the Amazon rainforest, there is a scientific lab and living compound called Little Cambridge. Little Cam houses some of the world's top scientific minds, including geneticists, biochemical engineers, botanists, and entomologists. <br /><br />It also hides a girl named Pia - seventeen years old, immortal, and <em>perfect</em>. <br /><br />The fruit of five generations of genetic experimentation and injection of a toxic, but immortality-giving extract from the rare bloom, elysia, Pia is the only one of her kind, and a scientific breakthrough that means the beginning of a new, immortal brand of humanity. Pia is the hope for humankind's future - she has perfect memory, razor-sharp reflexes, and superhuman speed and endurance. Her skin is impervious to harm, and cannot be penetrated by any blade, flame, or trauma. Every day of Pia's life, she has been told that she is perfect by the scientists that have raised her - yet, the thing she yearns for most of all, the right to join the Imortis Project and be embraced as a full-fledged scientist with the aim of creating a super-race of immortals like her, is still out of her reach. For all that Pia is perfect, she is not yet ready for whatever the Imortis Project entails, until she can pass a series of psychological and scientific tests. <br /><br />On the eve of Pia's eighteenth birthday, she discovers that the electric fence that surrounds Little Cam has a hole - and she does something she's never even dreamed of doing before. Buoyed by the confidence and good spirits of her first ever birthday party, Pia sneaks through the hole and braves the jungle outside for the first time...and she runs into a boy named Eio, and discovers an entirely new world. As she learns more about Eio and his tribe, the Ai'oa, Pia's view of her destiny changes, irrevocably. At what cost does immortality come? And is Pia ready to pay the price for eternity?<br /><br />The debut novel from an astonishingly young new author, <em>Origin</em> simultaneously succeeds and fails. There is no doubt that Jessica Khoury has a gift for writing and storytelling, as <em>Origin</em> is a compulsively readable and entertaining book. The novel begins on a high note, as we are introduced to our young immortal, impervious Pia: a <em>perfect</em> girl of "astonishing beauty" (as she is described by one character), whose very existence is the fruit of careful genetic selection and breeding for five consecutive generations. Pia is told, repeatedly, by almost everyone she knows, that she is perfect, and as such, she <em>believes</em> herself to be perfect. She's spoiled, self-entitled, and, well, bratty - especially to a perky new doctor that arrives on the scene, shortly before her eighteenth birthday. This initial characterization is immensely believable and well executed by Khoury - you can't help but feel exasperated with Pia's ingrained snobbery, but it rings as wholly genuine at the same time. When Pia chooses to escape the world of Little Cam - which has, for all intents and purposes, been a Pia-centered paradise as far as she is concerned - her world view radically changes, and she is challenged for the first time by her discovery of a boy named Eio and his tribe, the Ai'oa. This, unfortunately, is where the story slightly goes off the rails. <br /><br />But before we get to the negatives, the positives. On the plus side, I love Pia's dedication to <em>science</em>, and her devotion to fulfilling her destiny by creating a race of immortals. After all, this is the goal to which she's been attuned her entire life, and the prospect of eternity alone is certainly no fun. There's a palpable tension throughout <em>Origin</em>, and Pia's struggle to reconcile her predetermined path with her own emotions is exceptionally well done. The small, insular world of Little Cam and all that Pia knows feels <em>real</em>; the obsession of the scientists that have raised her (and the inevitable dark secrets they guard) are also genuinely engaging. Ultimately, <em>Origin</em> kinda reads like a super-hyperbolized metaphor for overprotected you girl, growing up and breaking free of the constraints imposed on her by elders that are acting in her best interest...sort of. (Of course, this particular young girl is immortal and incandescently beautiful, so the actual applicability of said metaphor - or, more tritely, self-insertionism - is dubious at best.)<br /><br />Praises said, <em>Origin</em> is not without some significant flaws. From a basic worldbuilding perspective, <em>Origin</em> is shaky, stumbling especially in its later chapters. This novel suffers from a problem of simplification and dichotomization - in <em>Origin</em>, this divide is (predictably) between <u>
  <strong>SCIENCE</strong>
</u> and <strong>
  <u>MORALITY</u>
</strong>. <u>
  <strong>SCIENCE</strong>
</u> is presented initially as Pia's God and creed: she has never heard music with lyrics, she has never read a novel (nor has she heard of the likes of Shakespeare or Plato), she has no knowledge of the world outside Little Cam. She <em>does</em> know the genus and species of all the plants and animals that surround her in the jungle, she can perform large mathematical sums in her head (though the bulk of this prowess is limited to multiplication, for whatever reason), and...she can sketch flowers pretty quickly (seriously).* <u>
  <strong>SCIENCE</strong>
</u>, of course, is revealed to be very, very bad, and Pia's immortality comes at the cost of countless lives - from the scientists who were forced to overcome their own moral compasses, and the blood literally shed to birth Pia. <br /><br />By contrast, Eio and the Ai'oa (and a couple select scientists) are the counterpoint of <strong>
  <u>MORALITY</u>
</strong> in the novel. The Ai'oa are rainforest NATIVES, and as such understand their immortality-giving flower better than the scientists ever could, and they teach Pia what it is to be a normal girl, to love, and to live. There's also a disturbing, continuing reference to the "natives" as being "ignorant," to the point where Eio (who is half-Ai'oan) repeatedly assets that he is not like his "ignorant" bretheren (i.e. "I <em>know</em> what electricity is...I am <em>not</em> an ignorant Ai'oan, Papi! Half of me belongs here in the jungle, yes, but half of me belongs on the other side of that fence with you and Pia!").<br /><br />And, speaking of Eio, there's the romance. OF COURSE when Pia slips out of Little Cam for the first time, the very first boy she runs into is Staggeringly Gorgeous. OF COURSE he also is half-caucasian, with striking blue eyes, chiseled features, standing head and shoulders above his Ai'oan village-mates (of course, to the Ai'oans, he is UGLY as is Pia). OF COURSE, Pia and Eio fall in insta-love - and the reason why? As Eio tells Pia, the first night he meets her:<br /><blockquote>"I lied when I said you were ugly. It is not true. You..."<br /><br />He scrubs at his hair, and his discomfort makes me smile.<br /><br />"You are in fact very beautiful. More beautiful than any girl I know."</blockquote><br />And then later:<br /><blockquote>"Ever since the moment you first knocked me over, then shone your stupid flashlight in my eyes and set your jaguar on me. I was angry, but mostly because I was terrified."<br /><br />"Am I really that scary?"<br /><br />"Your beauty is," he whispers.</blockquote><br />Cue excessive eye-rolling.** While it makes sense that Pia (and to some extent, Eio) is stunningly gorgeous - the progeny of generations of hand-selected beautiful people - the impetus for this Great Romance, based solely on the attractiveness of Pia and Eio, is a little silly, and a little insulting of readers' intelligence. <br /><br />Beyond these factors, consistency issues abound - Pia speaks in common slang and knows curses (e.g. she tells one character to screw himself at one point, she makes an internal joke about "going native" at another), but has <em>never</em> been around others who speak as such, nor has ever read a non-scientific book/seen a film before. The "science" in the later portion of the novel - the purpose of the tests that Pia has endured, the truth of the power of elysia and its catalyzing agent - are patently ridiculous, moving the book from dubious genetic possibility to complete science fantasy. <br /><br />And yet. For all of these flaws, I finished the book and I generally enjoyed myself, in a guilty pleasure, I-shouldn't-like-this-as-much-as-I-do kind of way. As long as one approaches the novel understanding that there are copious amounts of cheese and ridiculousness, there's something compulsively well-paced and guiltily engaging about <em>Origin</em>. Recommended, but leave expectations at the door.<br /><br />-------<br />*On a serious note, other than Pia's ididic memory and her ability to withstand sharp objects penetrating her skin, her skillset as an immortal heroine is somewhat disappointing.<br /><br />**It doesn't help that these characters are named Pia and Eio. When I read "Eio," I think Michael Jackson's version at Disneyland - which is infinitely more awesome than our love-struck jungle boy, who seems tohave no character or purpose, other than to wait around for Pia.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6818646376721348162" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText3983185784425137538" style="display:none">I'm torn. This was a phenomenally imaginative and beautifully narrated story of immortality and living life fully. The prose was captivating, the depictions vibrantly written. But the ending is hurting me - because it could have been so much better. But then I realize that it's more realistic for everything to not be all peaches and cream, which is most likely why the author concluded it this way. So that's why I'm torn. Because for the sake of the story and my feelings, a certain ending could have easily been written, but for the sake of not having a happily-ever-after novel, it had to be this way.</span>

<div id="review_1268000680" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1268000680" />
    <a title="Tilly Booth" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/23638789-tilly-booth"><img alt="Tilly Booth" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1530277607p2/23638789.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1268000680?book_show_action=true">Apr 30, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tilly Booth" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tilly Booth" href="/user/show/23638789-tilly-booth">Tilly Booth</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1268000680" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2469486383736239240">BOOK REVIEW: Origin by Jessica Khoury.<br/><br/>I really didn’t know what to expect from this book when I started which made me enjoy this book much more than others I know who have read it. Even though this book is a part of a series, it can be read as a stand-alone book. I wasn’t ready to get hooked on a series so this book was perfect and dragged me out of my “never-ending” slump.<br/><br/>I absolutely adored the science aspect of this book. I was hooked form the very beginning and I grew a thirst for knowledge.</span>
  <span id="freeText2469486383736239240" style="display:none">BOOK REVIEW: Origin by Jessica Khoury.<br /><br />I really didn’t know what to expect from this book when I started which made me enjoy this book much more than others I know who have read it. Even though this book is a part of a series, it can be read as a stand-alone book. I wasn’t ready to get hooked on a series so this book was perfect and dragged me out of my “never-ending” slump.<br /><br />I absolutely adored the science aspect of this book. I was hooked form the very beginning and I grew a thirst for knowledge. I wanted to know more and more about the creation of Immortals!<br /><br />The setting for this novel fascinated me too. The Amazon jungle and the way it was described were amazing. I could envision exactly how the author wanted me to picture it. And I have to mention the wildlife. I think one of my favorite “characters” would have to be Alai the panther.<br /><br />The characters were a slight downfall for me. There were parts with the main character, Pia that didn’t seem to flow. In the book she does explain herself as having two sides but she seemed to switch between “wild Pia” and “sane Pia” so often I didn’t know which one she really was. As for the love interest Eio, he was likeable. Simply and typically written. I would’ve really loved to have seen his character become much more developed and extra depth. Even though Pia is described as being “Perfect” it was Eio that seemed perfect to me. He didn’t do anything wrong. He says the right things. And that just wasn’t enough for me.<br /><br />With having so many characters in this book, the author did a good job at having contrast between them all. There are so many uncles and aunts that sometimes I couldn’t remember which ones were which.<br /><br />All in all though I did enjoy this book. Although the characters can seem generic, the story line is not. It’s brutal, beautiful and action filled.<br /><br />I give Origin by Jessica Khoury 3.5 stars out 5.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2469486383736239240" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_352275597" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/352275597" />
    <a title="Karen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2800496-karen"><img alt="Karen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1308338443p2/2800496.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/352275597?book_show_action=true">Jun 20, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Karen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Karen" href="/user/show/2800496-karen">Karen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer352275597" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6820461782819319012">This book was AWESOME!! There's something truly captivating about this story, I was not able to put the book down! Origin is a fantastic YA debut by author Jessica Khoury that will keep you frantically flipping the pages until you reach the end... and thinking about it long after you've put the book down.<br/><br/>Origin introduces us to Pia, an immortal created at Little Cam, a scientific community hidden in the middle of the rainforest. Pia is perfect, and has been told so her entire existence. Her skin</span>
  <span id="freeText6820461782819319012" style="display:none">This book was AWESOME!! There's something truly captivating about this story, I was not able to put the book down! Origin is a fantastic YA debut by author Jessica Khoury that will keep you frantically flipping the pages until you reach the end... and thinking about it long after you've put the book down.<br /><br />Origin introduces us to Pia, an immortal created at Little Cam, a scientific community hidden in the middle of the rainforest. Pia is perfect, and has been told so her entire existence. Her skin is impenetrable, she never gets sick, her reflexes and senses are extremely advanced, her memory is flawless, and her intelligence is off the charts. Pia's entire life has been focused on training her to be ready to take over the Immortis project. She will be the one to usher in a new race, HER race of immortals. What else could she ever want?<br /><br />The answer to that is ANSWERS... and freedom. Pia has never been outside the fence that surrounds Little Cam and it is forbidden for anyone at the compound to tell her of anything of the outside world. Pia's curiosity gets the best of her when she sees a hole in the fence, a hole that allows her to escape into the jungle. Here she meets Eio, an extremely attractive native with an interesting genetic makeup of his own. As Pia learns more about his world, and the world of his people, she starts to question where she really belongs. Could she have a life that does not revolve around elysia and the Immortis project?<br /><br />Further complicating matters is shocking revelations that Pia uncovers about the true nature of the catalyst that granted her immortality. When she herself is given a task that horrifies her, will she be able to turn off her emotions and be the cold and dedicated scientist Paolo and the others require? Or is her humanity too great? Perfect is as perfect does in this amazing debut novel.<br /><br />*SQUEE* I love this book!!! It's captivating, engaging, full of intrigue, romance, and even horror at times. This one will really get you thinking about right and wrong, good and evil, and the price of scientific discovery. Pia is the kind of character you just can't help rooting for. She's been sheltered and had secrets kept from her during her entire existence and getting to see her break out of that mold is wonderful.<br /><br />Readers are taken on Pia's journey as she discovers everything there is to know about Little Cam and her role in the attainment of an immortal race. When it is all said and done, will she be able to make the right choice, even if the right choice means saying goodbye to everything she has ever known? And will falling in love open her eyes to a world of possibilities? All will be revealed!!<br /><br />I really can't gush about this book enough. Everyone should read it! It's fresh, unique, and one of those books you just want to sit down with a glass of wine and a good friend and dissect! If you're over 21 anyway! Seriously though, this book is made of win. Be sure to grab a copy when it releases in September. Enjoy! *XOXO*</span>
  <a data-text-id="6820461782819319012" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_296217203" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/296217203" />
    <a title="Lexie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5889376-lexie"><img alt="Lexie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1339363563p2/5889376.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/296217203?book_show_action=true">Mar 17, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lexie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lexie" href="/user/show/5889376-lexie">Lexie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5889376-lexie?shelf=introspective-philosophical-thingys">introspective-philosophical-thingys</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5889376-lexie?shelf=lets-add-a-dash-of-misogyny">lets-add-a-dash-of-misogyny</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5889376-lexie?shelf=beautiful-cover">beautiful-cover</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5889376-lexie?shelf=great-concept">great-concept</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5889376-lexie?shelf=awesomem-setting-is-awesome">awesomem-setting-is-awesome</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5889376-lexie?shelf=ehhhhhh">ehhhhhh</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5889376-lexie?shelf=insta-love-blech">insta-love-blech</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5889376-lexie?shelf=whiny-mc">whiny-mc</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer296217203" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15982948205684752596">I wanted to love this, and there was a lot to love, but there was also a lot to stop me from really falling for this novel.<br/><br/>On the one hand, there are many things to commend: <br/><br/>It's clear Khoury did very extensive research; she didn't just decide, <i>oh, well, I think a jungle would be all</i> cool <i>and </i>mysterious. She researched it very thoroughly, and it shows. <br/><br/>As part of the Breathless Reads, this is labeled <i>Breathless Beauty</i>, and I can see why. At times, the writing was exceptionally beautiful; dramati</span>
  <span id="freeText15982948205684752596" style="display:none">I wanted to love this, and there was a lot to love, but there was also a lot to stop me from really falling for this novel.<br /><br />On the one hand, there are many things to commend: <br /><br />It's clear Khoury did very extensive research; she didn't just decide, <i>oh, well, I think a jungle would be all</i> cool <i>and </i>mysterious. She researched it very thoroughly, and it shows. <br /><br />As part of the Breathless Reads, this is labeled <i>Breathless Beauty</i>, and I can see why. At times, the writing was exceptionally beautiful; dramatic or poignant or just plain enchanting. <br /><br />While some of the mysteries verged on predictable, they were enough of a pull to keep me turning the pages well into the early hours of the morning, and some of their reveals were genuinely horrifying. They made for a very intriguing, very complex plot.<br /><br />And as several blurbs and the synopsis itself imply, at times, this is very introspective. It did genuinely make me think, and I love when a book can do that.<br /><br />But despite all of that, there were just too many things that soured my reading experience. There's insta-love at its finest; though they don't kiss till the very end, there's dramatic declarations of love and <i>I-can't-live-without-you's</i> within days of knowing each other. I'm sorry, but that's not love, that's <i>You're attractive and different and I think we should swap saliva.</i> I might have been slightly more forgiving of it if I'd really adored Eio, but . . . I didn't. He was cheesy, melodramatic, and occasionally misogynistic. He had his moments, but he never really found a place in my heart. <br /><br />Also, I could never tell exactly what sort of significance the romance was supposed to have. It was as though Khoury couldn't decide whether she wanted it to be a side plot or the main story line; even after finishing, I'm still not entirely sure which it is.<br /><br />Along with the heavy dose of insta-love, there were also a number of other problems. The dialogue was exceptionally unrealistic. Certain parts were so forced and melodramatic that I could only cringe. While the writing could be beautiful, at times it grew awkward and juvenile. Pia was sometimes really fantastic, but most of the time she was very naive, wishy-washy, and oblivious. And all the villains of this piece were painfully one-dimensional. That was perhaps the most disappointing, because I think with this concept there's the potential to create exceptionally complex villains and raise some very morally ambiguous questions, but all of the antagonists were just . . . bad. Plain and simple. The novel was a field of black and white, without a shade of gray to be seen. The evil scientists vs. the moral natives. I wanted some more depth.<br /><br />Overall, this novel was enjoyable, but there were just a few too many things dragging it down. However, I think Jessica Khoury has a boatload of potential, and I'll definitely be on the lookout for any future novels.<br /><br />2.5 stars.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15982948205684752596" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Flora Smith" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2811313-flora-smith"><img alt="Flora Smith" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1548294616p2/2811313.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/445437528?book_show_action=true">Oct 31, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Flora Smith" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Flora Smith" href="/user/show/2811313-flora-smith">Flora Smith</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2811313-flora-smith?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2811313-flora-smith?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2811313-flora-smith?shelf=romance">romance</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer445437528" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2169712481540506215">I loved this read! Was tempted to give this a 5 star rating but decided I couldn't quite stretch my rating that high. There is so much going on in this book and the story keeps you tied to it til the very end where everything is wrapped up nicely as it should be. <br/><br/>Pia is an immortal girl, born in the jungle in a secret laboratory. She is bio-engineered to be what she is and is only one of her kind. Raised in the jungle, she is kept away from the outside world. Not only is she not allowed to ventu</span>
  <span id="freeText2169712481540506215" style="display:none">I loved this read! Was tempted to give this a 5 star rating but decided I couldn't quite stretch my rating that high. There is so much going on in this book and the story keeps you tied to it til the very end where everything is wrapped up nicely as it should be. <br /><br />Pia is an immortal girl, born in the jungle in a secret laboratory. She is bio-engineered to be what she is and is only one of her kind. Raised in the jungle, she is kept away from the outside world. Not only is she not allowed to venture out of Little Cam but she isn't allowed to learn anything about the world outside. But teens thirsty for knowing what they've been told they shouldn't know about tend to find a way. With the help and the love of Eio her eyes are opened to the world around her.<br /><br />Pia is a wonderful character. She is so real that we feel her turmoil with the decisions that she is forced to make. She's so torn between what she is and what she wants to be and she has to learn for herself what is right and what is wrong. Eio is a wonderful match to Pia. He brings out the part of her that wants to be free and know the trutch. Their love is a love that all of us wish for. <br /><br />The story line itself also deals with so many different issues espcially with the scientific community. Just like with Mary Shelly's Frankenstein, just becuase we can do something does that mean we should? What price is too high for the quest for knowledge? And when we get that knowledge what do we do with it? <br /><br />Overall, I loved this book. I would recommend this to anyone that loves a YA read with a sci/fi twist to it. Its a wonderful stand alone novel, and even though the epilogue gives a chance for a second novel I hope it doesn't happen. This book is great as is. </span>
  <a data-text-id="2169712481540506215" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6990538-lauren?shelf=september-2012">september-2012</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer319944243" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11813345633003860309">The two books I read immediately prior to this were thrilling YA romances - entertaining, but quickly forgotten - and I was sort of expecting more of the same. Origin completely exceeded my expectations, and I was surprised by the level of depth it achieved - at least for me. <br/><br/>Origin deals with some pretty heavy themes, and because of this I think it'll be a love/hate kind of book. Some will definitely find the approach heavy-handed or uncomfortable in its depiction of the 'natives' and scientist</span>
  <span id="freeText11813345633003860309" style="display:none">The two books I read immediately prior to this were thrilling YA romances - entertaining, but quickly forgotten - and I was sort of expecting more of the same. Origin completely exceeded my expectations, and I was surprised by the level of depth it achieved - at least for me. <br /><br />Origin deals with some pretty heavy themes, and because of this I think it'll be a love/hate kind of book. Some will definitely find the approach heavy-handed or uncomfortable in its depiction of the 'natives' and scientists, but it worked for me (or I at least was able to forgive this) because I really connected with Pia on an emotional level. Some of the religious issues explored are ones I've dealt with a lot growing up, and I've been wanting to read a book like this for a while. For me, at least, it was very thought-provoking and amazingly relevant to my life, and books like these are exactly why I love YA no matter how controversial: it's amazing to be able to read a book that captures, explores, and makes ENTERTAINING the challenges and angst of growing up.<br /><br />Was this book perfect? Of course not, and many other reviewers have already pointed out some of its flaws. But as Khoury addresses in Origin, both in the book's opening line and repeatedly throughout, perfect is in the eye of the beholder, and Origin, at this point in my life, was absolutely perfect for me. LOVED it, and unlike some of the other YA I've read recently, I find myself still thinking about it a day later.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11813345633003860309" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer344556948" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3887538016174411649">4.5 but I'm rounding up. I was lucky enough to get an ARC of this book and I still can't get over how much I loved it. The beginning was a little slow for me which is why I'm giving it 4.5 and not 5. I think what I loved most about this book is that it is stand alone. These days, authors of YA fiction have been dragging out stories, which is great because it gets teens to read, but at the same time, it's very hard to commit to an entire series. Origin was great because it was excellent in its ow</span>
  <span id="freeText3887538016174411649" style="display:none">4.5 but I'm rounding up. I was lucky enough to get an ARC of this book and I still can't get over how much I loved it. The beginning was a little slow for me which is why I'm giving it 4.5 and not 5. I think what I loved most about this book is that it is stand alone. These days, authors of YA fiction have been dragging out stories, which is great because it gets teens to read, but at the same time, it's very hard to commit to an entire series. Origin was great because it was excellent in its own right. There was no wait to read the conclusion. Khoury tied Pia and Eio's story together perfectly, but it wasn't just them. She really tied up most of the loose ends (with the exception of that epilogue which might be a jumping point into another book if this one does well). <br /><br />She did a terrific job of making the reader connect with Pia and really empathize with her. Obviously, I'm not immortal, but I was able to understand how Pia felt about her immortality and what was driving all of her decisions throughout the book. I love that she was thrown the curveball that is Eio (I still want to know how to pronounce his name, btw) and it was completely fitting that this 17 year old's world is tilted askew by a cute boy and new older sister figure (Harriet Fields). That end was slightly predictable, but more because it was what I was hoping for. The way Khoury did it though, and the whole thought/understanding process Pia goes through was terrific. Highly recommend.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3887538016174411649" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_281741484" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/281741484" />
    <a title="Ceitidh" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3850693-ceitidh"><img alt="Ceitidh" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1469631051p2/3850693.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/281741484?book_show_action=true">Feb 21, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ceitidh" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ceitidh" href="/user/show/3850693-ceitidh">Ceitidh</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3850693-ceitidh?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3850693-ceitidh?shelf=2012">2012</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3850693-ceitidh?shelf=arc">arc</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer281741484" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6131872434577216222"><b>Read more of my reviews at <a href='http://dazzlingreads.blogspot.com/' rel='nofollow noopener'>Dazzling Reads</a></b><br/><br/> <b>Vividly Imagined, Compelling and Highly Refreshing!</b><br/><br/> Wow, where should I start? Jessica Khoury's debut novel impressed me to the core! Origin is unique and marvelous story where science, magic, reason and emotions intertwine to deliver a well-thought-out story with moral and philosophical content.<br/><br/> Origin takes place in the middle of the Amazon rainforest. Here, a group of scientists have establish a small community in order to find the flower containing a</span>
  <span id="freeText6131872434577216222" style="display:none"><b>Read more of my reviews at <a href="http://dazzlingreads.blogspot.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">Dazzling Reads</a></b><br /><br /> <b>Vividly Imagined, Compelling and Highly Refreshing!</b><br /><br /> Wow, where should I start? Jessica Khoury's debut novel impressed me to the core! Origin is unique and marvelous story where science, magic, reason and emotions intertwine to deliver a well-thought-out story with moral and philosophical content.<br /><br /> Origin takes place in the middle of the Amazon rainforest. Here, a group of scientists have establish a small community in order to find the flower containing an elixir capable to give immortality. However, only after more than a century after constant research and studies, scientists have finally been able to create the perfect first immortal, Pia. Here in the middle of the Amazon, Pia is isolated from the rest of the world. She has never been taught about other countries or anything that could raise her curiosity and make her want to live the scientific research camp... until on day she is able to make it out of her "cage" and goes into the jungle for the first time and meet with the first boy around her age she has ever seen.<br /><br /> Origin is a thoughtful well thought-out story and I was gladly surprised to have find a great deal of philosophical content on this novel. The fact that Khoury approached immortality in such a thoughtful way is what I love the most about Origin. What if we could get rid of sickness and mortality? what if we could create a raze of perfect and immortals human beings? What is perfection? What would it mean to become like God? Khoury brilliantly approaches all of these questions through the raw emotions of Pia. In addition, Pia is an unforgettable character whose reason keeps forcing her to obey, yet her heart never cease to question her captivity and origin. Pia's emotional side brings many other themes into this novel that wonderfully contrasts the scientific side of the story. Freedom, love, family, lies, and sens of belonging are some of the themes that Origin wonderfully deliver through Pia's life.<br /><br /> There is no doubt that Jessica Khoury is a highly talented writer with a vivid and exclusive imagination. Origin 's setting is marvelous and terrifying at the same time. The presence of the powerful elements and raw and wild rainforest took my breath away countless of times. Also, the amazonian people (the Ai'oans) are the delightful addition to the story that brings magic and mythology that beautifully intertwines with the scientific content.<br /><br /> Origin is a vividly imagined, compelling and highly refreshing debut that provides much more than just entertainment to its readers. Needless to say that the Epilogue alone will leave readers staring into space for quiet a while after they turned the last page.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6131872434577216222" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_417344754" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/417344754" />
    <a title="Shannon (Giraffe Days)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/395599-shannon-giraffe-days"><img alt="Shannon (Giraffe Days)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1420332941p2/395599.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/417344754?book_show_action=true">Sep 18, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Shannon (Giraffe Days)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Shannon (Giraffe Days)" href="/user/show/395599-shannon-giraffe-days">Shannon (Giraffe Days)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/395599-shannon-giraffe-days?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/395599-shannon-giraffe-days?shelf=ya">ya</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/395599-shannon-giraffe-days?shelf=2012">2012</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer417344754" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2791004009574934961">Pia is perfect, and she knows it. It's what the scientists at Little Cam have called her since she was born, and they make no secret of her origins. Pia is no ordinary sixteen year old: she has a photographic memory, has cat-like reflexes, and impervious skin. She cannot be killed, and will live forever. But more than that: she's being trained as a scientist so that she can run the Immortis program, creating more like her, starting with a man she can mate with. Little Cam is a big secret, a high</span>
  <span id="freeText2791004009574934961" style="display:none">Pia is perfect, and she knows it. It's what the scientists at Little Cam have called her since she was born, and they make no secret of her origins. Pia is no ordinary sixteen year old: she has a photographic memory, has cat-like reflexes, and impervious skin. She cannot be killed, and will live forever. But more than that: she's being trained as a scientist so that she can run the Immortis program, creating more like her, starting with a man she can mate with. Little Cam is a big secret, a high-security compound deep in the Amazon jungle, staffed by twenty-four scientists, a dozen guards and various cleaners, cooks and various others. The scientists all work on different projects, but they're all there in the Amazon for the Elysia flower, which produces a toxic nectar that can also bring everlasting life via a catalyst.<br /><br />Pia is surrounded by people who mostly treat her as something special and wondrous, but she feels terribly alone. She's completely focused on joining the Immortis team so that she can create someone like her, but first she has to go through a series of tests to make sure she's ready - tests that involve her inflicting pain and even death of living creatures. Her sense of morality comes mostly from Uncle Paolo, the leader of the scientists at Little Cam, who has taught her that emotions are a weakness, that nothing is more important than a scientists' cool rational thinking mind.<br /><br />But Pia is curious about what's on the other side of the fence that surrounds Little Cam. She's never been allowed outside, having been taught that people would fear her and hurt her. She's not even allowed to know the names of cities in the world, or about other cultures. She studies the sciences and mathematics, but her textbooks are heavily censored. On the night of her seventeenth birthday, though, Pia discovers that a hole in the fence, and slips through unnoticed. In the jungle, she meets Eio, a half-breed tribesman from the nearby Ai'oa village, and is welcomed into their homes.<br /><br />Through her friendship with Eio, and because of some questions posed by the new scientist, Dr Harriet Fields, Pia begins to question things in a new way, and notice things she never really paid attention to before. What is the catalyst for the Immortis injection? What is the purpose of B Labs, which she has always been told was locked up due to damage from a fire but that turns out to be a lie. When she discovers the truth of Little Cam and her own existence, everything changes and Pia must make the biggest decision of her life.<br /><br />This standalone, debut novel from Jessica Khoury has its strengths and its disappointments. It falls into some old traps while at the same time striving for something fresh, something new and different. As a story, to read without really thinking about it, it's very well written, and Khoury shows great potential. It's certainly better written than plenty of other YA books I've read in recent years, and there's a greater sense of the author being in control of her work. The setting, kept small and confined, is believable and figures large in your imagination. Pia, our narrator, is likewise believable. And I did enjoy the story, quite a lot.<br /><br />Where <i>Origin</i> loses traction is in its very structure. It was a strong choice to create this micro world for Pia, where she is spoilt and self-centred and has a narrow perception of the world - she was created that way. But she has a skewed sense of morality, with the idea being - learned from the scientists - that morality is weakness, that it impedes true scientific breakthroughs. Little Cam is a secret, and as such it is far removed from any ethics committee. They see their work as one of sacrifice, in more ways than one. While this creates a fascinating role for Pia in terms of character development - it truly is a strength of the novel to watch her grow and change, question things and weigh choices in terms of other people, not just herself - it also undermines itself through a polarisation that harks back to centuries-old debates of science versus religion, only here it's not religion but morality. <br /><br />This black-and-white polarisation works tremendously well for the narrative, the plot and the character of Pia, but like any black-and-white extremes, it oversimplifies the issue and thus renders the story as a whole too simplistic. And sadly, it's not the only cliched trope it employs. The one that really made my eyes roll was Eio. Sweet boy, lovely character, but meeting him, while explained quite well, still seemed a huge coincidence, and I couldn't really believe that he would fall in love with Pia so easily, so quickly. And while the fact that he's mulatto is part of the plot, it still grated on me - mostly his blue eyes bothered me (also, I always thought his father wasn't white - can't say what I thought he was without giving it away, but definitely not white). But like I said, it did work for the plot, and Eio is a bridge between Pia and the Ai'oa, and enables the story to go where it goes, in a way that him being fully native wouldn't have. <br /><br />The other trouble was that, despite this being told in Pia's voice, their chemistry isn't very strong. With the stakes being as high as they are, I would expect to <i>feel</i> Pia's emotions, even repressed, much more vividly. I suppose she never really loses her cool scientific mind, and there's nothing wrong with that - we don't all feel or express emotions to the same degree, and that doesn't make them weaker or false. But Pia could have shown more feeling than she did, and I would have expected her to be more troubled and confused than she was, given that it was all new to her.<br /><br />Even though I didn't particularly like the science vs. everything else polarisation, it did still serve a purpose, and is sadly rather true to life, in the setting up of the scientists as colonisers, invading another land to commandeer a precious resource for its own purposes, and exploiting the original inhabitants along the way. While these days it's mostly big business that does that - oil and mining companies would be the most obvious guilty parties, though governments in general are often behind them - it is an effective way of highlighting it, in the microcosm of Pia's world. Not to mention the fact that, big business and big money are behind it - someone has to fund the project, after all.<br /><br />I had a fun time trying to figure things out for myself, with mixed results, which I love - I never <i>try</i> to predict a book, most of the time, and it always disappoints me when a story is way too obvious in its direction and "twists". I still figured it out before Pia did - does that make me smarter than a so-called genius? Just kidding. I have the advantage of world knowledge, cynicism, suspicion and understanding people better than Pia, who lives in a bubble world. The one major flaw in Pia's character is how she speaks, which is just like how a teen character in a story set in any contemporary American urban setting would speak. She uses some expressions and colloquialisms that she would never have learned from the scientists, who come from all over the world and are a serious, maudlin bunch. There should have been a greater contrast than there was, when Harriet Fields arrives and shakes up Pia's world a bit.<br /><br />Without a doubt, the strength of this novel lies in Pia's transformation, as well as the carefully crafted atmosphere that gradually becomes charged with mistrust, suspicion, and danger. The story shows what happens to people when we go to an extreme and lack balance in life, and are unchecked by an uninvolved third party. It speaks well to our need for regulation in pharmaceuticals, among other things. It shows how dangerous ignorance can be, as well as how dangerous unchecked ambition can be, and it shows the price paid by people who derive no benefit from their invaders. But it's also the story of a girl facing an eternity alone, and how she deals with the knowledge of her own self, especially when her feelings develop in conflict with her life purpose. As I said before, it works on some levels, and where it works, it has great strength. And the use of present tense - a growing pet peeve of mine - didn't bother me overmuch.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2791004009574934961" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('8par1C+MyB8FlDc4efDEJ4lc4nh/axzXHUaJhHHH6U6yUB9DHYVyd8EZ+Rmbe8rzv6u+suCphZv7rrb4/bolqA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('SPjUkHF3O//zS9Mg79gLlB4bPQncscuvbtqDc5Ek3qIIPmAHQ36BlzfGHQENUwVAKOxhw0NzUuOIMrwPHVkSRA==')})
  }
//]]>

<span id="freeTextContainer16062194113544505576">Jessica Khoury wrote her first book at age 4, a fan fic sequel to Syd Hoff's Danny and the Dinosaur, which she scribbled on notebook paper, stapled together, and placed on the bookshelf of her preschool classroom. Since that day, she's dreamed of being an author.<br/> <br/>When not writing, Jess enjoys spending time with family, playing video games, and oil and watercolor painting. She is also a professiona</span>
  <span id="freeText16062194113544505576" style="display:none">Jessica Khoury wrote her first book at age 4, a fan fic sequel to Syd Hoff's Danny and the Dinosaur, which she scribbled on notebook paper, stapled together, and placed on the bookshelf of her preschool classroom. Since that day, she's dreamed of being an author.<br /> <br />When not writing, Jess enjoys spending time with family, playing video games, and oil and watercolor painting. She is also a professional mapmaker, and spends far too much time scribbling tiny trees and mountains for fictional worlds.<br /> <br />Jess currently lives in Greenville, South Carolina. She is the author of the Corpus trilogy, The Forbidden Wish, Last of Her Name, and The Mystwick School of Musicraft.</span>
